“So that’s it,” Anya said. Her face was pale, etched with exhaustion and a profound, unanswerable calm. “The god is dead. The machine is off. The briefcase is gone.” She looked at him. “What’s left, Leo?”

He had no answer. He had been a weapon, a shield, a key. Now he was a man on a mountain in the snow. The scar on his eyebrow ached with the cold.

Kaela was staring down the slope, towards where the valley lay hidden. “The geothermal instability… without the entity’s field to regulate it… it will vent. Possibly catastrophically. This entire region could become uninhabitable for decades. A dead zone.”

“A fitting monument,” Anya said, her voice hollow.

The wind shifted, carrying a new sound up from below. Not the mountain’s pain. A mechanical thrum, growing steadily louder. A familiar, muted scream against the weather’s howl.

A shape resolved itself from the grey-white roar: sleek, carbon-fibre grey, moving with predatory grace against the storm. A Gulfstream G700. It was not landing. It was circling, a vulture assessing a corpse.

Leo watched it, his eyes the colour of the wet slate beneath the snow. He’d stopped seeing beauty in machines like this years ago. Now he saw only persistence. The system was a hydra. You cut off the head of a god in a mountain, and a corporate jet appeared in the sky, drawn by the silence of a dead signal, the void left by extinguished power. The resource allocation was immediate, obscene. The sheer, obscene amount of will it represented.

“Silas’s people,” Kaela said. “Or the Board’s contingency. They’ll have thermal imaging. They’ll see us.”

“Let them see,” Leo said.

The jet banked, turning a wide circle around the peak. It was not leaving.

Anya moved to stand beside him, her shoulder almost touching his. “You asked me once what came after the fire. This is it. The cold. The quiet. The people who come to sift the ashes.”

Leo looked from the circling jet to his daughter’s face. She was not looking at the plane. She was looking at him, waiting. Kaela stood a pace apart, a solitary figure, the blood on her temple black in the flat light. Both scarred by the same beautiful, terrible dream.

The corruption was purged. The power was broken. All that remained was the consequence.

And the consequence had a jet, and satellite links, and a hunger for whatever scraps of power might remain. They would want a debrief. An assessment. They would want to know if the asset was recoverable, if the research was salvageable. They would want to contain the narrative, as they had once tried to contain the light. They would see three survivors, and they would see variables. Loose ends.

The wind tore at them, carrying the scent of snow and jet fuel.

“We can’t stay here,” Kaela said, stating the obvious with the last of her logistical mind.

“No,” Leo agreed.

He looked up at the sky, at the circling shadow. Then he looked down the mountain, into the white oblivion of the slopes below. There were no paths. Only descent.

He had fought the system from the outside, then tried to steer it from within, then become the poison that shut it down. He was the contradiction. But a contradiction only exists within a structure. Out here, in the cold and the wind, there was no logic to negate. There was only the next step. The next choice.

He took a step away from the ledge, towards the treacherous, open slope. Then another.

“We go,” he said, the words snatched away by the gale, tasting of ice and beginnings.

Anya followed without a word. Kaela, after a last, long look at the circling jet, fell in behind.

They moved into the white, leaving the high ground and the watching eye above, three dark specks dissolving into the storm’s embrace. The mountain trembled beneath them, a deep, subterranean shockwave—the first of the convulsions to come. The sky darkened further, the snow thickening, a shroud falling over the wound in the world.

They descended into the consequence, a vast, cold landscape with no maps, where the only certainty was the hunger of the world they had broken, and the quieter, more terrible hunger of the one waiting to take its place. The spiral had turned. They were back in the weather. But they were not who they had been.

And above it all, the sleek, grey shape continued its patient, silent circle, a promise against the grey-white roar of the sky.
